<p>The Mississippi basin is home to a highly diverse aquatic <a href="/wiki/Fauna" title="Fauna">fauna</a> and has been called the "mother fauna" of North American freshwater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews1998_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews1998-111">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fish">Fish</span></h3>
<p>About 375 fish species are known from the Mississippi basin, far exceeding other North Hemisphere river basins exclusively within temperate/subtropical regions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews1998_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews1998-111">[110]</a></sup> except the <a href="/wiki/Yangtze" title="Yangtze">Yangtze</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup> Within the Mississippi basin, streams that have their source in the Appalachian and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ozark" title="Ozark">Ozark</a> highlands contain especially many species. Among the fish species in the basin are numerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endemic" title="Endemic">endemics</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Relict_(biology)" title="Relict (biology)">relicts</a> such as <a href="/wiki/American_paddlefish" title="American paddlefish">paddlefish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sturgeon" title="Sturgeon">sturgeon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gar" title="Gar">gar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bowfin" title="Bowfin">bowfin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthews1998_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews1998-111">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of its size and high species diversity, the Mississippi basin is often divided into subregions. The Upper Mississippi River alone is home to about 120 fish species, including <a href="/wiki/Walleye" title="Walleye">walleye</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sauger" title="Sauger">sauger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Largemouth_bass" title="Largemouth bass">largemouth bass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smallmouth_bass" title="Smallmouth bass">smallmouth bass</a>, <a href="/wiki/White_bass" title="White bass">white bass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_pike" title="Northern pike">northern pike</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bluegill" title="Bluegill">bluegill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crappie" title="Crappie">crappie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Channel_catfish" title="Channel catfish">channel catfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flathead_catfish" title="Flathead catfish">flathead catfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_shiner" title="Common shiner">common shiner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Freshwater_drum" title="Freshwater drum">freshwater drum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shovelnose_sturgeon" title="Shovelnose sturgeon">shovelnose sturgeon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_fauna">Other fauna</span></h3>
<p>A large number of reptiles are native to the river channels and basin, including <a href="/wiki/American_alligator" title="American alligator">American alligators</a>, several species of turtle, aquatic amphibians,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cambaridae" title="Cambaridae">cambaridae</a> crayfish, are native to the Mississippi basin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition, approximately 40% of the <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migratory birds</a> in the US use the Mississippi River corridor during Spring and Fall migrations; 60% of all migratory birds in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> (326 species) use the river basin as their flyway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduced_species">Introduced species</span></h3>
<p>Numerous <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">introduced species</a> are found in the Mississippi and some of these are <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasive</a>. Among the introductions are fish such as <a href="/wiki/Asian_carp_in_North_America" title="Asian carp in North America">Asian carp</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Silver_carp" title="Silver carp">silver carp</a> that have become infamous for out-competing native fish and their potentially dangerous <a href="/wiki/Asian_carp_in_North_America#Jumping_ability" title="Asian carp in North America">jumping behavior</a>. They have spread throughout much of the basin, even approaching (but not yet invading) the Great Lakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Minnesota_Department_of_Natural_Resources" title="Minnesota Department of Natural Resources">Minnesota Department of Natural Resources</a> has designated much of the Mississippi River in the state as infested waters by the exotic species <a href="/wiki/Zebra_mussel" title="Zebra mussel">zebra mussels</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eurasian_watermilfoil" title="Eurasian watermilfoil">Eurasian watermilfoil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">United States Geological Survey</a> recognizes two contrasting definitions of a river's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Source_(river_or_stream)" title="Source (river or stream)">source</a>.<a class="external text" href="http://pubs.usgs.gov/of/1987/ofr87-242/" rel="nofollow">USGS.gov</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20170630064901/https://pubs.usgs.gov/of/1987/ofr87-242/" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> June 30, 2017, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> By the stricter definition, the Mississippi would share its source with its longest tributary, the Missouri, at <a href="/wiki/Brower%27s_Spring" title="Brower's Spring">Brower's Spring</a> in Montana. The other definition acknowledges "somewhat arbitrary decisions" and places the Mississippi's source at Lake Itasca, which is publicly accepted as the source,<a class="external text" href="http://pubs.usgs.gov/of/1987/ofr87-242/" rel="nofollow">USGS.gov</a>  and which had been identified as such by <a href="/wiki/Jacob_V._Brower" title="Jacob V. Brower">Brower</a> himself.<a class="external text" href="http://fwp.mt.gov/mtoutdoors/HTML/articles/2005/MissouriSource.htm" rel="nofollow">MT.gov</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20120118010715/http://fwp.mt.gov/mtoutdoors/HTML/articles/2005/MissouriSource.htm" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> January 18, 2012, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> However, the river continues for several miles upstream from Lake Itasca to <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nicolet_Lake&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nicolet Lake (page does not exist)">Nicolet Lake</a> and its feeder stream.</span>
</li>
